   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Greenhouse gas
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
     <div class="flaggedrevs_short flaggedrevs_stable_synced plainlinks noprint nomobile" id="mw-fr-revisiontag">
      <div class="flaggedrevs_short_basic">
       <img alt="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page." class="flaggedrevs-icon" src="/w/extensions/FlaggedRevs/frontend/modules/img/doc-magnify.png" title="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page."/>
       <img alt="show/hide details" class="fr-toggle-arrow" id="mw-fr-revisiontoggle" src="/w/extensions/FlaggedRevs/frontend/modules/img/arrow-down.png" style="display:none;"/>
      </div>
      <div id="mw-fr-revisiondetails-wrapper" style="position:relative;">
       <div class="flaggedrevs_short_details" id="mw-fr-revisiondetails" style="display:none">
        This is the
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Pending_changes" title="Wikipedia:Pending changes">
         latest accepted revision
        </a>
        ,
        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Special:Log&amp;type=review&amp;page=Greenhouse_gas">
         reviewed
        </a>
        on
        <i>
         2 November 2016
        </i>

    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      For other uses, see
      <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/GHG_(disambiguation)" title="GHG (disambiguation)">
       GHG
      </a>
      .
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greenhouse_Effect.svg">
        <img alt="refer to caption and image description" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="577" data-file-width="750" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Greenhouse_Effect.svg/350px-Greenhouse_Effect.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Greenhouse_Effect.svg/525px-Greenhouse_Effect.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Greenhouse_Effect.svg/700px-Greenhouse_Effect.svg.png 2x" width="350"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greenhouse_Effect.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">
         Greenhouse effect
        </a>
        schematic showing
        <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">
         energy
        </a>
        flows between
        <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">
         space
        </a>
        , the
        <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">
         atmosphere
        </a>
        , and Earth's
        <a href="/wiki/Earth#Surface" title="Earth">
         surface
        </a>
        .
        <a href="/wiki/Irradiance" title="Irradiance">
         Energy influx and emittance
        </a>
        are expressed in
        <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">
         watts
        </a>
        per
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Square_meter" title="Square meter">
         square meter
        </a>
        (W/m
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
        ).
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      A
      <b>
       greenhouse gas
      </b>
      (abbrev.
      <b>
       GHG
      </b>
      ) is a
      <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">
       gas
      </a>
      in an atmosphere that
      <a href="/wiki/Absorption_(electromagnetic_radiation)" title="Absorption (electromagnetic radiation)">
       absorbs
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emission_(electromagnetic_radiation)" title="Emission (electromagnetic radiation)">
       emits
      </a>
      radiation within the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_infrared" title="Thermal infrared">
       thermal infrared
      </a>
      range. This process is the fundamental cause of the
      <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">
       greenhouse effect
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_AR4-SYR_1-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-IPCC_AR4-SYR-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The primary greenhouse gases in
      <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">
       Earth's atmosphere
      </a>
      are
      <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">
       water vapor
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
       carbon dioxide
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">
       methane
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">
       nitrous oxide
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">
       ozone
      </a>
      . Without greenhouse gases, the average temperature of
      <a href="/wiki/Earth#Surface" title="Earth">
       Earth's surface
      </a>
      would be about −18 °C (0 °F),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
      rather than the present average of 15 °C (59 °F).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        [3]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h2o_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-h2o-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">
       Solar System
      </a>
      , the atmospheres of
      <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Venus" title="Atmosphere of Venus">
       Venus
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars" title="Atmosphere of Mars">
       Mars
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Titan" title="Atmosphere of Titan">
       Titan
      </a>
      also contain gases that cause a greenhouse effect.
     </p>
     <p>
      Human activities since the beginning of the
      <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">
       Industrial Revolution
      </a>
      (taken as the year 1750) have produced a 40% increase in the
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere">
       atmospheric concentration of carbon dioxide
      </a>
      , from 280
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">
       ppm
      </a>
      in 1750 to 400 ppm in 2015.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blasing_ghg_concentrations_6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-blasing_ghg_concentrations-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This increase has occurred despite the uptake of a large portion of the emissions by various natural "sinks" involved in the
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">
       carbon cycle
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cdiac_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-cdiac-8">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">
       Anthropogenic
      </a>
      carbon dioxide (CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      ) emissions (i.e. emissions produced by human activities) come from
      <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">
       combustion
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon-based_fuel" title="Carbon-based fuel">
       carbon-based fuels
      </a>
      , principally
      <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">
       coal
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Oil" title="Oil">
       oil
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">
       natural gas
      </a>
      , along with deforestation, soil erosion and animal agriculture.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      It has been estimated that if greenhouse gas emissions continue at the present rate, Earth's surface temperature could exceed historical values as early as 2047, with potentially harmful
      <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming" title="Effects of global warming">
       effects on ecosystems, biodiversity and the livelihoods of people
      </a>
      worldwide.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        [11]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Recent estimates suggest that on the current emissions trajectory the Earth could pass a threshold of 2°C global warming, which the United Nations'
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IPCC" title="IPCC">
       IPCC
      </a>
      designated as the upper limit for "dangerous" global warming, by 2036.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        [12]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

          </li>
          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25">

     </div>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Gases_in_Earth.27s_atmosphere">
       Gases in Earth's atmosphere
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Gases in Earth's atmosphere">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">
       Greenhouse effect
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">
       Global warming
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere">
       Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere
      </a>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Greenhouse_gases">
       Greenhouse gases
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Greenhouse gases">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atmospheric_Transmission.png">
        <img alt="refer to caption and adjacent text" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="857" data-file-width="850" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Atmospheric_Transmission.png/220px-Atmospheric_Transmission.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Atmospheric_Transmission.png/330px-Atmospheric_Transmission.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Atmospheric_Transmission.png/440px-Atmospheric_Transmission.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Atmospheric_Transmission.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Atmospheric absorption and scattering at different
        <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">
         wavelengths
        </a>
        of
        <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">
         electromagnetic waves
        </a>
        . The largest absorption band of
        <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
         carbon dioxide
        </a>
        is in the
        <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">
         infrared
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Greenhouse gases are those that absorb and emit
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_infrared" title="Thermal infrared">
       infrared radiation in the wavelength range emitted by Earth
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_AR4-SYR_1-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-IPCC_AR4-SYR-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In order, the most abundant greenhouse gases in Earth's atmosphere are:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">
        Water vapor
       </a>
       (
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        H
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         2
        </span>
        O
       </span>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
        Carbon dioxide
       </a>
       (
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        CO
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         2
        </span>
       </span>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">
        Methane
       </a>
       (
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        CH
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         4
        </span>
       </span>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">
        Nitrous oxide
       </a>
       (
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        N
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         2
        </span>
        O
       </span>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">
        Ozone
       </a>
       (
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        O
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         3
        </span>
       </span>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbon" title="Chlorofluorocarbon">
        Chlorofluorocarbons
       </a>
       (CFCs)
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      Atmospheric concentrations of greenhouse gases are determined by the balance between sources (emissions of the gas from human activities and natural systems) and sinks (the removal of the gas from the atmosphere by conversion to a different chemical compound).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_WG1_AR4_Ch7_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-IPCC_WG1_AR4_Ch7-13">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The proportion of an emission remaining in the atmosphere after a specified time is the "
      <a href="/wiki/Airborne_fraction" title="Airborne fraction">
       airborne fraction
      </a>
      " (AF). More precisely, the annual airborne fraction is the ratio of the atmospheric increase in a given year to that year's total emissions. Over the last 50 years (1956–2006) the airborne fraction for CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      has been increasing at 0.25 ± 0.21%/year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canadell2007_14-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Canadell2007-14">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Non-greenhouse_gases">
       Non-greenhouse gases
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Non-greenhouse gases">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The major atmospheric constituents,
      <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">
       nitrogen
      </a>
      (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       N
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      ),
      <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">
       oxygen
      </a>
      (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      ), and
      <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">
       argon
      </a>
      (Ar), are not greenhouse gases because
      <a href="/wiki/Diatomic_molecule" title="Diatomic molecule">
       molecules containing two atoms of the same element
      </a>
      such as
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       N
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      and
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monatomic" title="Monatomic">
       monatomic
      </a>
      molecules such as argon (Ar) have no net change in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_dipole_moment" title="Molecular dipole moment">
       distribution of their electrical charges
      </a>
      when they vibrate and hence are almost totally unaffected by
      <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">
       infrared radiation
      </a>
      . Although molecules containing two atoms of different elements such as
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">
       carbon monoxide
      </a>
      (CO) or
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_chloride" title="Hydrogen chloride">
       hydrogen chloride
      </a>
      (HCl) absorb infrared radiation, these molecules are short-lived in the atmosphere owing to their reactivity and solubility. Therefore, they do not contribute significantly to the greenhouse effect and usually are omitted when discussing greenhouse gases.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Indirect_radiative_effects">
       Indirect radiative effects
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Indirect radiative effects">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mopitt_first_year_carbon_monoxide.jpg">
        <img alt="world map of carbon monoxide concentrations in the lower atmosphere" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="630" data-file-width="540" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Mopitt_first_year_carbon_monoxide.jpg/200px-Mopitt_first_year_carbon_monoxide.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Mopitt_first_year_carbon_monoxide.jpg/300px-Mopitt_first_year_carbon_monoxide.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Mopitt_first_year_carbon_monoxide.jpg/400px-Mopitt_first_year_carbon_monoxide.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mopitt_first_year_carbon_monoxide.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The false colors in this image represent concentrations of carbon monoxide in the lower atmosphere, ranging from about 390 parts per billion (dark brown pixels), to 220 parts per billion (red pixels), to 50 parts per billion (blue pixels).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
         <a href="#cite_note-15">
          [15]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Some gases have indirect radiative effects (whether or not they are greenhouse gases themselves). This happens in two main ways. One way is that when they break down in the atmosphere they produce another greenhouse gas. For example, methane and carbon monoxide (CO) are oxidized to give carbon dioxide (and methane oxidation also produces water vapor; that will be considered below). Oxidation of CO to CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      directly produces an unambiguous increase in radiative forcing although the reason is subtle. The peak of the thermal IR emission from Earth's surface is very close to a strong vibrational absorption band of CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      (667 cm
      <sup>
       −1
      </sup>
      ). On the other hand, the single CO vibrational band only absorbs IR at much higher frequencies (2145 cm
      <sup>
       −1
      </sup>
      ), where the ~300 K thermal emission of the surface is at least a factor of ten lower. On the other hand, oxidation of methane to CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      , which requires reactions with the OH radical, produces an instantaneous reduction, since CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      is a weaker greenhouse gas than methane; but it has a longer lifetime. As described below this is not the whole story, since the oxidations of CO and
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       CH
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        4
       </span>
      </span>
      are intertwined by both consuming OH radicals. In any case, the calculation of the total radiative effect needs to include both the direct and indirect forcing.
     </p>
     <p>
      A second type of indirect effect happens when chemical reactions in the atmosphere involving these gases change the concentrations of greenhouse gases. For example, the destruction of
      <a href="/wiki/Non-methane_volatile_organic_compound" title="Non-methane volatile organic compound">
       non-methane volatile organic compounds
      </a>
      (NMVOCs) in the atmosphere can produce ozone. The size of the indirect effect can depend strongly on where and when the gas is emitted.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forsteretal_16-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-forsteretal-16">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Methane has a number of indirect effects in addition to forming CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      . Firstly, the main chemical that destroys methane in the atmosphere is the
      <a href="/wiki/Hydroxyl_radical" title="Hydroxyl radical">
       hydroxyl radical
      </a>
      (OH). Methane reacts with OH and so more methane means that the concentration of OH goes down. Effectively, methane increases its own atmospheric lifetime and therefore its overall radiative effect. The second effect is that the oxidation of methane can produce ozone. Thirdly, as well as making CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      the oxidation of methane produces water; this is a major source of water vapor in the
      <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">
       stratosphere
      </a>
      , which is otherwise very dry. CO and NMVOC also produce CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      when they are oxidized. They remove OH from the atmosphere and this leads to higher concentrations of methane. The surprising effect of this is that the global warming potential of CO is three times that of CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maccartyetal_17-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-maccartyetal-17">
        [17]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The same process that converts NMVOC to carbon dioxide can also lead to the formation of tropospheric ozone. Halocarbons have an indirect effect because they destroy stratospheric ozone. Finally
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
       hydrogen
      </a>
      can lead to ozone production and
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       CH
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        4
       </span>
      </span>
      increases as well as producing water vapor in the stratosphere.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forsteretal_16-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-forsteretal-16">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Contribution_of_clouds_to_Earth.27s_greenhouse_effect">
       Contribution of clouds to Earth's greenhouse effect
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Contribution of clouds to Earth's greenhouse effect">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The major non-gas contributor to Earth's greenhouse effect,
      <a href="/wiki/Cloud_forcing" title="Cloud forcing">
       clouds
      </a>
      , also absorb and emit infrared radiation and thus have an effect on radiative properties of the greenhouse gases. Clouds are water droplets or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ice_crystal" title="Ice crystal">
       ice crystals
      </a>
      suspended in the atmosphere.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kiehl197_18-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-kiehl197-18">
        [18]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-realclimate.org_19-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-realclimate.org-19">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Impacts_on_the_overall_greenhouse_effect">
       Impacts on the overall greenhouse effect
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Impacts on the overall greenhouse effect">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Attribution_of_individual_atmospheric_component_contributions_to_the_terrestrial_greenhouse_effect,_separated_into_feedback_and_forcing_categories_(NASA).png">
        <img alt="refer to caption and adjacent text" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2333" data-file-width="3266" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Attribution_of_individual_atmospheric_component_contributions_to_the_terrestrial_greenhouse_effect%2C_separated_into_feedback_and_forcing_categories_%28NASA%29.png/220px-Attribution_of_individual_atmospheric_component_contributions_to_the_terrestrial_greenhouse_effect%2C_separated_into_feedback_and_forcing_categories_%28NASA%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Attribution_of_individual_atmospheric_component_contributions_to_the_terrestrial_greenhouse_effect%2C_separated_into_feedback_and_forcing_categories_%28NASA%29.png/330px-Attribution_of_individual_atmospheric_component_contributions_to_the_terrestrial_greenhouse_effect%2C_separated_into_feedback_and_forcing_categories_%28NASA%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Attribution_of_individual_atmospheric_component_contributions_to_the_terrestrial_greenhouse_effect%2C_separated_into_feedback_and_forcing_categories_%28NASA%29.png/440px-Attribution_of_individual_atmospheric_component_contributions_to_the_terrestrial_greenhouse_effect%2C_separated_into_feedback_and_forcing_categories_%28NASA%29.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Attribution_of_individual_atmospheric_component_contributions_to_the_terrestrial_greenhouse_effect,_separated_into_feedback_and_forcing_categories_(NASA).png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Schmidt
        <i>
         et al.
        </i>
        (2010)
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
         <a href="#cite_note-20">
          [20]
         </a>
        </sup>
        analysed how individual components of the atmosphere contribute to the total greenhouse effect. They estimated that water vapor accounts for about 50% of Earth's greenhouse effect, with clouds contributing 25%, carbon dioxide 20%, and the minor greenhouse gases and
        <a href="/wiki/Aerosol" title="Aerosol">
         aerosols
        </a>
        accounting for the remaining 5%. In the study, the reference model atmosphere is for 1980 conditions. Image credit:
        <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
         NASA
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
         <a href="#cite_note-21">
          [21]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The contribution of each gas to the greenhouse effect is affected by the characteristics of that gas, its abundance, and any indirect effects it may cause. For example, the direct radiative effect of a mass of methane is about 72 times stronger than the same mass of carbon dioxide over a 20-year time frame
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TableOfWarmingPotentials_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-TableOfWarmingPotentials-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      but it is present in much smaller concentrations so that its total direct radiative effect is smaller, in part due to its shorter atmospheric lifetime. On the other hand, in addition to its direct radiative impact, methane has a large, indirect radiative effect because it contributes to ozone formation. Shindell
      <i>
       et al.
      </i>
      (2005)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        [23]
       </a>
      </sup>
      argue that the contribution to climate change from methane is at least double previous estimates as a result of this effect.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        [24]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      When ranked by their direct contribution to the greenhouse effect, the most important are:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kiehl197_18-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-kiehl197-18">
        [18]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
         <span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (April 2016)">
          not in citation given
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>

     </table>
     <p>
      In addition to the main greenhouse gases listed above, other greenhouse gases include
      <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_hexafluoride" title="Sulfur hexafluoride">
       sulfur hexafluoride
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrofluorocarbon" title="Hydrofluorocarbon">
       hydrofluorocarbons
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perfluorocarbon" title="Perfluorocarbon">
       perfluorocarbons
      </a>
      (see
      <a href="/wiki/IPCC_list_of_greenhouse_gases" title="IPCC list of greenhouse gases">
       IPCC list of greenhouse gases
      </a>
      ). Some greenhouse gases are not often listed. For example,
      <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_trifluoride" title="Nitrogen trifluoride">
       nitrogen trifluoride
      </a>
      has a high
      <a href="/wiki/Global_warming_potential" title="Global warming potential">
       global warming potential
      </a>
      (GWP) but is only present in very small quantities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NF3_27-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NF3-27">
        [27]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Proportion_of_direct_effects_at_a_given_moment">
       Proportion of direct effects at a given moment
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Proportion of direct effects at a given moment">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      It is not possible to state that a certain gas causes an exact percentage of the greenhouse effect. This is because some of the gases absorb and emit radiation at the same frequencies as others, so that the total greenhouse effect is not simply the sum of the influence of each gas. The higher ends of the ranges quoted are for each gas alone; the lower ends account for overlaps with the other gases.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kiehl197_18-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-kiehl197-18">
        [18]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-realclimate.org_19-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-realclimate.org-19">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In addition, some gases such as methane are known to have large indirect effects that are still being quantified.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Atmospheric_lifetime">
       Atmospheric lifetime
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Atmospheric lifetime">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Aside from
      <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">
       water vapor
      </a>
      , which has a
      <a href="/wiki/Residence_time_(fluid_dynamics)" title="Residence time (fluid dynamics)">
       residence time
      </a>
      of about nine days,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      major greenhouse gases are well mixed and take many years to leave the atmosphere.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-betts_30-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-betts-30">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although it is not easy to know with precision how long it takes greenhouse gases to leave the atmosphere, there are estimates for the principal greenhouse gases. Jacob (1999)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JacobDJ1999_31-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-JacobDJ1999-31">
        [31]
       </a>
      </sup>
      defines the lifetime
      <span>
       <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
        <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
         <semantics>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
           <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
            <mi>
             τ
             <!-- τ -->
            </mi>
           </mstyle>
          </mrow>
          <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
           {\displaystyle \tau }
          </annotation>
         </semantics>
        </math>
       </span>
       <img alt="\tau " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/38a7dcde9730ef0853809fefc18d88771f95206c" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.212ex; height:1.676ex;"/>
      </span>
      of an atmospheric
      <a href="/wiki/Chemical_species" title="Chemical species">
       species
      </a>
      X in a one-
      <a href="/wiki/Climate_model#Box_models" title="Climate model">
       box model
      </a>
      as the average time that a molecule of X remains in the box. Mathematically
      <span>
       <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
        <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
         <semantics>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
           <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
            <mi>
             τ
             <!-- τ -->
            </mi>
           </mstyle>
          </mrow>
          <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
           {\displaystyle \tau }
          </annotation>
         </semantics>
        </math>
       </span>
       <img alt="\tau " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/38a7dcde9730ef0853809fefc18d88771f95206c" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.212ex; height:1.676ex;"/>
      </span>
      can be defined as the ratio of the mass
      <span>
       <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
        <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
         <semantics>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
           <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
            <mi>
             m
            </mi>
           </mstyle>
          </mrow>
          <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
           {\displaystyle m}
          </annotation>
         </semantics>
        </math>
       </span>
       <img alt="m" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0a07d98bb302f3856cbabc47b2b9016692e3f7bc" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.051ex; height:1.676ex;"/>
      </span>
      (in kg) of X in the box to its removal rate, which is the sum of the flow of X out of the box (
      <span>
       <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
        <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
         <semantics>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
           <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
            <msub>
             <mi>
              F
             </mi>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mi>
               o
              </mi>
              <mi>
               u
              </mi>
              <mi>
               t
              </mi>
             </mrow>
            </msub>
           </mstyle>
          </mrow>
          <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
           {\displaystyle F_{out}}
          </annotation>
         </semantics>
        </math>
       </span>
       <img alt="F_{{out}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/010777ed1b8fe2ac62e41ec617400d95065c5bec" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:4.091ex; height:2.509ex;"/>
      </span>
      ), chemical loss of X (
      <span>
       <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
        <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
         <semantics>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
           <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
            <mi>
             L
            </mi>
           </mstyle>
          </mrow>
          <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
           {\displaystyle L}
          </annotation>
         </semantics>
        </math>
       </span>
       <img alt="L" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/103168b86f781fe6e9a4a87b8ea1cebe0ad4ede8" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.593ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
      </span>
      ), and
      <a href="/wiki/Deposition_(chemistry)" title="Deposition (chemistry)">
       deposition
      </a>
      of X (
      <span>
       <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
        <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
         <semantics>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
           <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
            <mi>
             D
            </mi>
           </mstyle>
          </mrow>
          <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
           {\displaystyle D}
          </annotation>
         </semantics>
        </math>
       </span>
       <img alt="D" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f34a0c600395e5d4345287e21fb26efd386990e6" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.935ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
      </span>
      ) (all in kg/s):
      <span>
       <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
        <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
         <semantics>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
           <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
            <mi>
             τ
             <!-- τ -->
            </mi>
            <mo>
             =
            </mo>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mfrac>
              <mi>
               m
              </mi>
              <mrow>
               <msub>
                <mi>
                 F
                </mi>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mi>
                  o
                 </mi>
                 <mi>
                  u
                 </mi>
                 <mi>
                  t
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
               </msub>
               <mo>
                +
               </mo>
               <mi>
                L
               </mi>
               <mo>
                +
               </mo>
               <mi>
                D
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </mfrac>
            </mrow>
           </mstyle>
          </mrow>
          <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
           {\displaystyle \tau ={\frac {m}{F_{out}+L+D}}}
          </annotation>
         </semantics>
        </math>
       </span>
       <img alt="\tau ={\frac  {m}{F_{{out}}+L+D}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/181e5432246a85f54af3b8a2defb06fa88fe8d38" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:18.478ex; height:5.176ex;"/>
      </span>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JacobDJ1999_31-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-JacobDJ1999-31">
        [31]
       </a>
      </sup>
      If one stopped pouring any of this gas into the box, then after a time
      <span>
       <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
        <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
         <semantics>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
           <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
            <mi>
             τ
             <!-- τ -->
            </mi>
           </mstyle>
          </mrow>
          <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
           {\displaystyle \tau }
          </annotation>
         </semantics>
        </math>
       </span>
       <img alt="\tau " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/38a7dcde9730ef0853809fefc18d88771f95206c" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.212ex; height:1.676ex;"/>
      </span>
      , its concentration would be about halved.
     </p>
     <p>
      The atmospheric lifetime of a species therefore measures the time required to restore equilibrium following a sudden increase or decrease in its concentration in the atmosphere. Individual atoms or molecules may be lost or deposited to sinks such as the soil, the oceans and other waters, or vegetation and other biological systems, reducing the excess to background concentrations. The average time taken to achieve this is the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mean_lifetime" title="Mean lifetime">
       mean lifetime
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
       Carbon dioxide
      </a>
      has a variable atmospheric lifetime, and cannot be specified precisely.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        [32]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The atmospheric lifetime of CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      is estimated of the order of 30–95 years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JacobsonMZ2005_33-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-JacobsonMZ2005-33">
        [33]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This figure accounts for CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      molecules being removed from the atmosphere by mixing into the ocean, photosynthesis, and other processes. However, this excludes the balancing fluxes of CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      into the atmosphere from the geological reservoirs, which have slower characteristic rates.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArcherD2009_34-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ArcherD2009-34">
        [34]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although more than half of the CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      emitted is removed from the atmosphere within a century, some fraction (about 20%) of emitted CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      remains in the atmosphere for many thousands of years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        [35]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carbon_lifetime1_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-carbon_lifetime1-36">
        [36]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carbon_lifetime2_37-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-carbon_lifetime2-37">
        [37]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Similar issues apply to other greenhouse gases, many of which have longer mean lifetimes than CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      . E.g.,
      <a href="#N2O">
       N
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       O
      </a>
      has a mean atmospheric lifetime of 114 years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TableOfWarmingPotentials_22-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-TableOfWarmingPotentials-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Radiative_forcing">
       Radiative forcing
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Radiative forcing">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Earth absorbs some of the radiant energy received from the sun, reflects some of it as light and reflects or radiates the rest back to space as
      <a href="/wiki/Thermal_radiation" title="Thermal radiation">
       heat
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa_radiative_forcing_38-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-epa_radiative_forcing-38">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Earth's surface temperature depends on this balance between incoming and outgoing energy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa_radiative_forcing_38-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-epa_radiative_forcing-38">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
      If this
      <a href="/wiki/First_law_of_thermodynamics" title="First law of thermodynamics">
       energy balance
      </a>
      is shifted, Earth's surface could become warmer or cooler, leading to a variety of changes in global climate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa_radiative_forcing_38-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-epa_radiative_forcing-38">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A number of natural and man-made mechanisms can affect the global energy balance and force changes in Earth's climate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa_radiative_forcing_38-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-epa_radiative_forcing-38">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Greenhouse gases are one such mechanism.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa_radiative_forcing_38-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-epa_radiative_forcing-38">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Greenhouse gases in the atmosphere absorb and re-emit some of the outgoing energy radiated from Earth's surface, causing that heat to be retained in the lower atmosphere.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa_radiative_forcing_38-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-epa_radiative_forcing-38">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
      As
      <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas#Atmospheric_lifetime" title="Greenhouse gas">
       explained above
      </a>
      , some greenhouse gases remain in the atmosphere for decades or even centuries, and therefore can affect Earth's energy balance over a long time period.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa_radiative_forcing_38-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-epa_radiative_forcing-38">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Factors that influence Earth's energy balance can be quantified in terms of "
      <a href="/wiki/Radiative_forcing" title="Radiative forcing">
       radiative climate forcing
      </a>
      ."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa_radiative_forcing_38-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-epa_radiative_forcing-38">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Positive radiative forcing indicates warming (for example, by increasing incoming energy or decreasing the amount of energy that escapes to space), whereas negative forcing is associated with cooling.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa_radiative_forcing_38-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-epa_radiative_forcing-38">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Global_warming_potential">
       Global warming potential
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Global warming potential">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Global_warming_potential" title="Global warming potential">
       global warming potential
      </a>
      (GWP) depends on both the efficiency of the molecule as a greenhouse gas and its atmospheric lifetime. GWP is measured relative to the same
      <b>
       mass
      </b>
      of CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      and evaluated for a specific timescale. Thus, if a gas has a high (positive)
      <a href="/wiki/Radiative_forcing" title="Radiative forcing">
       radiative forcing
      </a>
      but also a short lifetime, it will have a large GWP on a 20-year scale but a small one on a 100-year scale. Conversely, if a molecule has a longer atmospheric lifetime than CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      its GWP will increase with the timescale considered. Carbon dioxide is defined to have a GWP of 1 over all time periods.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">
       Methane
      </a>
      has an atmospheric lifetime of 12 ± 3 years. The
      <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Fourth_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Fourth Assessment Report">
       2007 IPCC report
      </a>
      lists the GWP as 72 over a time scale of 20 years, 25 over 100 years and 7.6 over 500 years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TableOfWarmingPotentials_22-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-TableOfWarmingPotentials-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A 2014 analysis, however, states that although methane's initial impact is about 100 times greater than that of CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      , because of the shorter atmospheric lifetime, after six or seven decades, the impact of the two gases is about equal, and from then on methane's relative role continues to decline.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        [39]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The decrease in GWP at longer times is because
      <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_methane#Removal_processes" title="Atmospheric methane">
       methane
      </a>
      is degraded to water and CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      through chemical reactions in the atmosphere.
     </p>
     <p>
      Examples of the atmospheric lifetime and
      <a href="/wiki/Global_warming_potential" title="Global warming potential">
       GWP
      </a>
      relative to CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      for several greenhouse gases are given in the following table:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TableOfWarmingPotentials_22-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-TableOfWarmingPotentials-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <table class="wikitable" style="text-align: right">

      </caption>

      <a href="/wiki/Haloalkane" title="Haloalkane">

       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        [41]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_and_anthropogenic_sources">
       Natural and anthropogenic sources
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Natural and anthropogenic sources">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_History_and_Flux_Rev.png">
        <img alt="refer to caption and article text" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="642" height="274" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Carbon_History_and_Flux_Rev.png/220px-Carbon_History_and_Flux_Rev.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Carbon_History_and_Flux_Rev.png/330px-Carbon_History_and_Flux_Rev.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Carbon_History_and_Flux_Rev.png/440px-Carbon_History_and_Flux_Rev.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_History_and_Flux_Rev.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Top: Increasing atmospheric
        <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
         carbon dioxide
        </a>
        levels as measured in the atmosphere and reflected in
        <a href="/wiki/Ice_core" title="Ice core">
         ice cores
        </a>
        . Bottom: The amount of net carbon increase in the atmosphere, compared to carbon emissions from burning
        <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">
         fossil fuel
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diagram_showing_a_simplified_representation_of_the_Earth%27s_annual_carbon_cycle_(US_DOE).png">
        <img alt="refer to caption and image description" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2192" data-file-width="2600" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Diagram_showing_a_simplified_representation_of_the_Earth%27s_annual_carbon_cycle_%28US_DOE%29.png/220px-Diagram_showing_a_simplified_representation_of_the_Earth%27s_annual_carbon_cycle_%28US_DOE%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Diagram_showing_a_simplified_representation_of_the_Earth%27s_annual_carbon_cycle_%28US_DOE%29.png/330px-Diagram_showing_a_simplified_representation_of_the_Earth%27s_annual_carbon_cycle_%28US_DOE%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Diagram_showing_a_simplified_representation_of_the_Earth%27s_annual_carbon_cycle_%28US_DOE%29.png/440px-Diagram_showing_a_simplified_representation_of_the_Earth%27s_annual_carbon_cycle_%28US_DOE%29.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diagram_showing_a_simplified_representation_of_the_Earth%27s_annual_carbon_cycle_(US_DOE).png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        This diagram shows a simplified representation of the contemporary global
        <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">
         carbon cycle
        </a>
        . Changes are measured in
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigaton" title="Gigaton">
         gigatons
        </a>
        of carbon per year (GtC/y). Canadell
        <i>
         et al.
        </i>
        (2007) estimated the growth rate of global average atmospheric CO
        <sub>
         2
        </sub>
        for 2000–2006 as 1.93 parts-per-million per year (4.1
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petagram" title="Petagram">
         petagrams
        </a>
        of carbon per year).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
         <a href="#cite_note-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Aside from purely human-produced synthetic halocarbons, most greenhouse gases have both natural and human-caused sources. During the pre-industrial
      <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">
       Holocene
      </a>
      , concentrations of existing gases were roughly constant. In the industrial era, human activities have added greenhouse gases to the atmosphere, mainly through the burning of fossil fuels and clearing of forests.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        [46]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The 2007
      <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Fourth_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Fourth Assessment Report">
       Fourth Assessment Report
      </a>
      compiled by the IPCC (AR4) noted that "changes in atmospheric concentrations of greenhouse gases and aerosols, land cover and solar radiation alter the energy balance of the climate system", and concluded that "increases in anthropogenic greenhouse gas concentrations is very likely to have caused most of the increases in global average temperatures since the mid-20th century".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        [48]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In AR4, "most of" is defined as more than 50%.
     </p>
     <p>
      <i>
       Abbreviations used in the two tables below: ppm =
       <a href="/wiki/Parts-per_notation" title="Parts-per notation">
        parts-per-million
       </a>
       ; ppb = parts-per-billion; ppt = parts-per-trillion; W/m
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       =
       <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">
        watts
       </a>
       per
       <a href="/wiki/Square_metre" title="Square metre">
        square metre
       </a>
      </i>
     </p>
     <table class="wikitable">
      <caption>
       Current greenhouse gas concentrations
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blasing_ghg_concentrations_6-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-blasing_ghg_concentrations-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </caption>
      <tr>
       <th>
        Gas
       </th>
       <th>
        Pre-1750
        <br/>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tropospheric" title="Tropospheric">
         tropospheric
        </a>
        <br/>
        concentration
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blasing_pre_1750_ghg_conc_49-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-blasing_pre_1750_ghg_conc-49">
          [49]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </th>
       <th>
        Recent
        <br/>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tropospheric" title="Tropospheric">
         tropospheric
        </a>
        <br/>
        concentration
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
         <a href="#cite_note-50">
          [50]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </th>
