     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vostok_Petit_data.svg">
        <img alt="refer to caption and article text" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Vostok_Petit_data.svg/220px-Vostok_Petit_data.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Vostok_Petit_data.svg/330px-Vostok_Petit_data.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Vostok_Petit_data.svg/440px-Vostok_Petit_data.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vostok_Petit_data.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        400,000 years of ice core data
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Ice_core" title="Ice core">
       Ice cores
      </a>
      provide evidence for greenhouse gas concentration variations over the past 800,000 years (see the
      <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas#Ice_cores" title="Greenhouse gas">
       following section
      </a>
      ). Both CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      and
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       CH
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        4
       </span>
      </span>
      vary between glacial and interglacial phases, and concentrations of these gases correlate strongly with temperature. Direct data does not exist for periods earlier than those represented in the ice core record, a record that indicates CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      <a href="/wiki/Mole_fraction" title="Mole fraction">
       mole fractions
      </a>
      stayed within a range of 180 ppm to 280 ppm throughout the last 800,000 years, until the increase of the last 250 years. However, various proxies and modeling suggests larger variations in past epochs; 500 million years ago CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      levels were likely 10 times higher than now.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Indeed, higher CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      concentrations are thought to have prevailed throughout most of the
      <a href="/wiki/Phanerozoic" title="Phanerozoic">
       Phanerozoic
      </a>
      eon, with concentrations four to six times current concentrations during the Mesozoic era, and ten to fifteen times current concentrations during the early Palaeozoic era until the middle of the
      <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">
       Devonian
      </a>
      period, about 400
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annum" title="Annum">
       Ma
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berner1994_60-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Berner1994-60">
        [60]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Royeretal2001_61-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Royeretal2001-61">
        [61]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berner.26Kothavala2001_62-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Berner.26Kothavala2001-62">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The spread of land plants is thought to have reduced CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      concentrations during the late Devonian, and plant activities as both sources and sinks of CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      have since been important in providing stabilising feedbacks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beerling2005_63-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Beerling2005-63">
        [63]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Earlier still, a 200-million year period of intermittent, widespread glaciation extending close to the equator (
      <a href="/wiki/Snowball_Earth" title="Snowball Earth">
       Snowball Earth
      </a>
      ) appears to have been ended suddenly, about 550 Ma, by a colossal volcanic outgassing that raised the CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      concentration of the atmosphere abruptly to 12%, about 350 times modern levels, causing extreme greenhouse conditions and carbonate deposition as
      <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">
       limestone
      </a>
      at the rate of about 1 mm per day.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffmannetal1998_64-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hoffmannetal1998-64">
        [64]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This episode marked the close of the Precambrian eon, and was succeeded by the generally warmer conditions of the Phanerozoic, during which multicellular animal and plant life evolved. No volcanic carbon dioxide emission of comparable scale has occurred since. In the modern era, emissions to the atmosphere from volcanoes are only about 1% of emissions from human sources.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffmannetal1998_64-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hoffmannetal1998-64">
        [64]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gerlach1991_65-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-gerlach1991-65">
        [65]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        [66]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ice_cores">
       Ice cores
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Ice cores">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere#Past_variation" title="Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere">
       Measurements from Antarctic ice cores
      </a>
      show that before industrial emissions started atmospheric CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      mole fractions were about 280
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">
       parts per million
      </a>
      (ppm), and stayed between 260 and 280 during the preceding ten thousand years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        [67]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Carbon dioxide mole fractions in the atmosphere have gone up by approximately 35 percent since the 1900s, rising from 280 parts per million by volume to 387 parts per million in 2009. One study using evidence from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stomata" title="Stomata">
       stomata
      </a>
      of fossilized leaves suggests greater variability, with carbon dioxide mole fractions above 300 ppm during the period seven to ten thousand years ago,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        [68]
       </a>
      </sup>
      though others have argued that these findings more likely reflect calibration or contamination problems rather than actual CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      variability.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        [69]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        [70]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Because of the way air is trapped in ice (pores in the ice close off slowly to form bubbles deep within the firn) and the time period represented in each ice sample analyzed, these figures represent averages of atmospheric concentrations of up to a few centuries rather than annual or decadal levels.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Changes_since_the_Industrial_Revolution">
       Changes since the Industrial Revolution
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Changes since the Industrial Revolution">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CO2_increase_rate.png">
        <img alt="Refer to caption" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="690" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/CO2_increase_rate.png/220px-CO2_increase_rate.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/CO2_increase_rate.png/330px-CO2_increase_rate.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/CO2_increase_rate.png/440px-CO2_increase_rate.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CO2_increase_rate.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Recent year-to-year increase of atmospheric CO
        <sub>
         2
        </sub>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Major_greenhouse_gas_trends.png">
        <img alt="Refer to caption" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="678" data-file-width="1014" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Major_greenhouse_gas_trends.png/220px-Major_greenhouse_gas_trends.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Major_greenhouse_gas_trends.png/330px-Major_greenhouse_gas_trends.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Major_greenhouse_gas_trends.png/440px-Major_greenhouse_gas_trends.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Major_greenhouse_gas_trends.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Major greenhouse gas trends.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Since the beginning of the
      <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">
       Industrial Revolution
      </a>
      , the concentrations of most of the greenhouse gases have increased. For example, the mole fraction of carbon dioxide has increased from 280 ppm by about 36% to 380 ppm, or 100 ppm over modern pre-industrial levels. The first 50 ppm increase took place in about 200 years, from the start of the Industrial Revolution to around 1973.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="next cite only covers most recent 50 years (May 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      ; however the next 50 ppm increase took place in about 33 years, from 1973 to 2006.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        [71]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Recent data also shows that the concentration is increasing at a higher rate. In the 1960s, the average annual increase was only 37% of what it was in 2000 through 2007.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        [72]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Today, the stock of carbon in the atmosphere increases by more than 3 million tonnes per annum (0.04%) compared with the existing stock.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
         <span title="in what year was this measurement made? (April 2011)">
          clarification needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      This increase is the result of human activities by burning fossil fuels, deforestation and forest degradation in tropical and boreal regions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tarziu2011_73-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Tarziu2011-73">
        [73]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The other greenhouse gases produced from human activity show similar increases in both amount and rate of increase. Many observations are available online in a variety of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_Chemistry_Observational_Databases" title="Atmospheric Chemistry Observational Databases">
       Atmospheric Chemistry Observational Databases
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Anthropogenic_greenhouse_gases">
       Anthropogenic greenhouse gases
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Anthropogenic greenhouse gases">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:254px;max-width:254px">
       <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NOAA_Annual_Greenhouse_Gas_Index_2012.png">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="552" data-file-width="801" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/51/NOAA_Annual_Greenhouse_Gas_Index_2012.png/250px-NOAA_Annual_Greenhouse_Gas_Index_2012.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/51/NOAA_Annual_Greenhouse_Gas_Index_2012.png/375px-NOAA_Annual_Greenhouse_Gas_Index_2012.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/51/NOAA_Annual_Greenhouse_Gas_Index_2012.png/500px-NOAA_Annual_Greenhouse_Gas_Index_2012.png 2x" width="250"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         This graph shows changes in the annual greenhouse gas index (AGGI) between 1979 and 2011.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-annual_greenhouse_gas_index_74-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-annual_greenhouse_gas_index-74">
           [74]
          </a>
         </sup>
         The AGGI measures the levels of greenhouse gases in the atmosphere based on their ability to cause changes in Earth's climate.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-annual_greenhouse_gas_index_74-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-annual_greenhouse_gas_index-74">
           [74]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
       <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector,_1990-2005,_in_carbon_dioxide_equivalents_(EPA,_2010).png">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="610" data-file-width="670" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Global_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector%2C_1990-2005%2C_in_carbon_dioxide_equivalents_%28EPA%2C_2010%29.png/250px-Global_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector%2C_1990-2005%2C_in_carbon_dioxide_equivalents_%28EPA%2C_2010%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Global_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector%2C_1990-2005%2C_in_carbon_dioxide_equivalents_%28EPA%2C_2010%29.png/375px-Global_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector%2C_1990-2005%2C_in_carbon_dioxide_equivalents_%28EPA%2C_2010%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Global_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector%2C_1990-2005%2C_in_carbon_dioxide_equivalents_%28EPA%2C_2010%29.png/500px-Global_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector%2C_1990-2005%2C_in_carbon_dioxide_equivalents_%28EPA%2C_2010%29.png 2x" width="250"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         This bar graph shows global greenhouse gas emissions by sector from 1990 to 2005, measured in
         <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">
          carbon dioxide equivalents
         </a>
         .
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
          <a href="#cite_note-75">
           [75]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
       <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg/250px-Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg/375px-Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg/500px-Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg.png 2x" width="250"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         Modern global CO2 emissions from the burning of fossil fuels.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Since about 1750 human activity has increased the concentration of carbon dioxide and other greenhouse gases. Measured atmospheric concentrations of carbon dioxide are currently 100 ppm higher than pre-industrial levels.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        [76]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Natural sources of carbon dioxide are more than 20 times greater than sources due to human activity,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        [77]
       </a>
      </sup>
      but over periods longer than a few years natural sources are closely balanced by natural sinks, mainly photosynthesis of carbon compounds by plants and marine plankton. As a result of this balance, the atmospheric mole fraction of carbon dioxide remained between 260 and 280 parts per million for the 10,000 years between the end of the last glacial maximum and the start of the industrial era.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_AR4_WG1_Chapter_7_78-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-IPCC_AR4_WG1_Chapter_7-78">
        [78]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      It is likely that
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anthropogenic" title="Anthropogenic">
       anthropogenic
      </a>
      (i.e., human-induced) warming, such as that due to elevated greenhouse gas levels, has had a discernible influence on many physical and biological systems.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
       <a href="#cite_note-79">
        [79]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Future warming is projected to have a range of
      <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming" title="Effects of global warming">
       impacts
      </a>
      , including
      <a href="/wiki/Sea_level_rise" title="Sea level rise">
       sea level rise
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-projected_impacts_of_climate_change_80-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-projected_impacts_of_climate_change-80">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
      increased frequencies and severities of some
      <a href="/wiki/Extreme_weather" title="Extreme weather">
       extreme weather
      </a>
      events,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-projected_impacts_of_climate_change_80-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-projected_impacts_of_climate_change-80">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
      loss of
      <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_and_ecosystems" title="Climate change and ecosystems">
       biodiversity
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-projected_impacts_of_climate_change_by_sector_81-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-projected_impacts_of_climate_change_by_sector-81">
        [81]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and regional changes in
      <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_and_agriculture" title="Climate change and agriculture">
       agricultural productivity
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-projected_impacts_of_climate_change_by_sector_81-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-projected_impacts_of_climate_change_by_sector-81">
        [81]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The main sources of greenhouse gases due to human activity are:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       burning of
       <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">
        fossil fuels
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">
        deforestation
       </a>
       leading to higher carbon dioxide concentrations in the air. Land use change (mainly deforestation in the tropics) account for up to one third of total anthropogenic CO
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       emissions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_AR4_WG1_Chapter_7_78-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-IPCC_AR4_WG1_Chapter_7-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       livestock
       <a href="/wiki/Enteric_fermentation" title="Enteric fermentation">
        enteric fermentation
       </a>
       and manure management,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-livestock_82-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-livestock-82">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
       paddy
       <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">
        rice
       </a>
       farming, land use and wetland changes, pipeline losses, and covered vented landfill emissions leading to higher methane atmospheric concentrations. Many of the newer style fully vented septic systems that enhance and target the fermentation process also are sources of
       <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_methane" title="Atmospheric methane">
        atmospheric methane
       </a>
       .
      </li>
      <li>
       use of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbons" title="Chlorofluorocarbons">
        chlorofluorocarbons
       </a>
       (CFCs) in
       <a href="/wiki/Refrigeration" title="Refrigeration">
        refrigeration
       </a>
       systems, and use of CFCs and
       <a href="/wiki/Halomethane#Fire_extinguishing" title="Halomethane">
        halons
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Fire_extinguisher" title="Fire extinguisher">
        fire suppression
       </a>
       systems and manufacturing processes.
      </li>
      <li>
       agricultural activities, including the use of fertilizers, that lead to higher nitrous oxide (
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        N
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         2
        </span>
        O
       </span>
       ) concentrations.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      The seven sources of CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      from fossil fuel combustion are (with percentage contributions for 2000–2004):
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raupach_83-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Raupach-83">
        [83]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center">
      <tr>
       <th>
        Seven main fossil fuel
        <br/>
        combustion sources
       </th>
       <th>
        Contribution
        <br/>
        (%)
       </th>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td style="text-align:left;">
        Liquid fuels (e.g.,
        <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">
         gasoline
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Fuel_oil" title="Fuel oil">
         fuel oil
        </a>
        )
       </td>
       <td>
        36%
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td style="text-align:left;">
        Solid fuels (e.g.,
        <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">
         coal
        </a>
        )
       </td>
       <td>
        35%
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td style="text-align:left;">
        Gaseous fuels (e.g.,
        <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">
         natural gas
        </a>
        )
       </td>
       <td>
        20%
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td style="text-align:left;">
        <a href="/wiki/Cement" title="Cement">
         Cement
        </a>
        production
       </td>
       <td>
        3 %
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td style="text-align:left;">
        <a href="/wiki/Gas_flare" title="Gas flare">
         Flaring
        </a>
        gas industrially and at wells
       </td>
       <td>
        &lt; 1%
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td style="text-align:left;">
        Non-fuel hydrocarbons
       </td>
       <td>
        &lt; 1%
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td style="text-align:left;">
        "International
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bunker_fuel" title="Bunker fuel">
         bunker fuels
        </a>
        " of transport
        <br/>
        not included in national inventories
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schrooten2_84-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Schrooten2-84">
          [84]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
       <td>
        4 %
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
       Carbon dioxide
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">
       methane
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">
       nitrous oxide
      </a>
      (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       N
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
       O
      </span>
      ) and three groups of
      <a href="/wiki/Fluorinated_gases" title="Fluorinated gases">
       fluorinated gases
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_hexafluoride" title="Sulfur hexafluoride">
       sulfur hexafluoride
      </a>
      (
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       SF
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        6
       </span>
      </span>
      ),
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrofluorocarbons" title="Hydrofluorocarbons">
       hydrofluorocarbons
      </a>
      (HFCs), and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perfluorocarbons" title="Perfluorocarbons">
       perfluorocarbons
      </a>
      (PFCs)) are the major anthropogenic greenhouse gases,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grubb_kyoto_protocol_85-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-grubb_kyoto_protocol-85">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :147
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Environmental_issues:_essential_primary_sources._86-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Environmental_issues:_essential_primary_sources.-86">
        [86]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and are regulated under the
      <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol" title="Kyoto Protocol">
       Kyoto Protocol
      </a>
      international
      <a href="/wiki/Treaty" title="Treaty">
       treaty
      </a>
      , which came into force in 2005.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unfccc_kyoto_protocol_87-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-unfccc_kyoto_protocol-87">
        [87]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Emissions limitations specified in the Kyoto Protocol expired in 2012.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unfccc_kyoto_protocol_87-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-unfccc_kyoto_protocol-87">
        [87]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/2010_United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference" title="2010 United Nations Climate Change Conference">
       Cancún agreement
      </a>
      , agreed in 2010, includes voluntary pledges made by 76 countries to control emissions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cancun_agreement_88-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-cancun_agreement-88">
        [88]
       </a>
      </sup>
      At the time of the agreement, these 76 countries were collectively responsible for 85% of annual global emissions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cancun_agreement_88-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-cancun_agreement-88">
        [88]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Although
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CFCs" title="CFCs">
       CFCs
      </a>
      are greenhouse gases, they are regulated by the
      <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Protocol" title="Montreal Protocol">
       Montreal Protocol
      </a>
      , which was motivated by CFCs' contribution to
      <a href="/wiki/Ozone_depletion" title="Ozone depletion">
       ozone depletion
      </a>
      rather than by their contribution to global warming. Note that ozone depletion has only a minor role in greenhouse warming though the two processes often are confused in the media. On 15 October 2016, negotiators from over 170 nations meeting at the summit of the
      <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Environment_Programme" title="United Nations Environment Programme">
       United Nations Environment Programme
      </a>
      reached a legally-binding accord to phase out
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrofluorocarbons" title="Hydrofluorocarbons">
       hydrofluorocarbons
      </a>
      (HFCs) in an amendment to the
      <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Protocol" title="Montreal Protocol">
       Montreal Protocol
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
       <a href="#cite_note-89">
        [89]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
       <a href="#cite_note-90">
        [90]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        [91]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>

       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">

       </a>

       </span>

     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">
       Tourism
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Tourism">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      According to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNEP" title="UNEP">
       UNEP
      </a>
      global
      <a href="/wiki/Tourism" title="Tourism">
       tourism
      </a>
      is closely linked to
      <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">
       climate change
      </a>
      . Tourism is a significant contributor to the increasing concentrations of greenhouse gases in the atmosphere. Tourism accounts for about 50% of traffic movements. Rapidly expanding air traffic contributes about 2.5% of the production of CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      . The number of international travelers is expected to increase from 594 million in 1996 to 1.6 billion by 2020, adding greatly to the problem unless steps are taken to reduce emissions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
       <a href="#cite_note-92">
        [92]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Road_Haulage">
       Road Haulage
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Road Haulage">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The road haulage industry plays a part in production of CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      , contributing around 20% of the UK's total carbon emissions a year, with only the energy industry having a larger impact at around 39%.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
       <a href="#cite_note-93">
        [93]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Average carbon emissions within the haulage industry are falling—in the thirty-year period from 1977–2007, the carbon emissions associated with a 200-mile journey fell by 21 percent; NOx emissions are also down 87 percent, whereas journey times have fallen by around a third.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
       <a href="#cite_note-94">
        [94]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Due to their size,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heavy_goods_vehicle" title="Heavy goods vehicle">
       HGVs
      </a>
      often receive criticism regarding their CO2 emissions; however, rapid development in engine technology and fuel management is having a largely positive effect.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_water_vapor">
       Role of water vapor
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Role of water vapor">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BAMS_climate_assess_boulder_water_vapor_2002.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="425" data-file-width="775" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/BAMS_climate_assess_boulder_water_vapor_2002.png/350px-BAMS_climate_assess_boulder_water_vapor_2002.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/BAMS_climate_assess_boulder_water_vapor_2002.png/525px-BAMS_climate_assess_boulder_water_vapor_2002.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/BAMS_climate_assess_boulder_water_vapor_2002.png/700px-BAMS_climate_assess_boulder_water_vapor_2002.png 2x" width="350"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BAMS_climate_assess_boulder_water_vapor_2002.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Increasing water vapor in the stratosphere at Boulder, Colorado.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">
       Water vapor
      </a>
      accounts for the largest percentage of the greenhouse effect, between 36% and 66% for clear sky conditions and between 66% and 85% when including clouds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-realclimate.org_19-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-realclimate.org-19">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Water vapor concentrations fluctuate regionally, but human activity does not significantly affect water vapor concentrations except at local scales, such as near irrigated fields. The atmospheric concentration of vapor is highly variable and depends largely on temperature, from less than 0.01% in extremely cold regions up to 3% by mass in saturated air at about 32 °C.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
       <a href="#cite_note-95">
        [95]
       </a>
      </sup>
      (See
      <a href="/wiki/Relative_humidity#other_important_facts" title="Relative humidity">
       Relative humidity#other important facts
      </a>
      .)
     </p>
     <p>
      The average residence time of a water molecule in the atmosphere is only about nine days, compared to years or centuries for other greenhouse gases such as
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       CH
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        4
       </span>
      </span>
      and CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
       <a href="#cite_note-96">
        [96]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thus, water vapor responds to and amplifies effects of the other greenhouse gases. The
      <a href="/wiki/Clausius%E2%80%93Clapeyron_relation" title="Clausius–Clapeyron relation">
       Clausius–Clapeyron relation
      </a>
      establishes that more water vapor will be present per unit volume at elevated temperatures. This and other basic principles indicate that warming associated with increased concentrations of the other greenhouse gases also will increase the concentration of water vapor (assuming that the
      <a href="/wiki/Relative_humidity" title="Relative humidity">
       relative humidity
      </a>
      remains approximately constant; modeling and observational studies find that this is indeed so). Because water vapor is a greenhouse gas, this results in further warming and so is a "
      <a href="/wiki/Positive_feedback" title="Positive feedback">
       positive feedback
      </a>
      " that amplifies the original warming. Eventually other earth processes offset these positive feedbacks, stabilizing the global temperature at a new equilibrium and preventing the loss of Earth's water through a Venus-like
      <a href="/wiki/Runaway_greenhouse_effect" title="Runaway greenhouse effect">
       runaway greenhouse effect
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
       <a href="#cite_note-97">
        [97]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Direct_greenhouse_gas_emissions">
       Direct greenhouse gas emissions
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Direct greenhouse gas emissions">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Between the period 1970 to 2004, GHG emissions (measured in
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">
       CO
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       -equivalent
      </a>
      )
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
       <a href="#cite_note-98">
        [98]
       </a>
      </sup>
      increased at an average rate of 1.6% per year, with CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      emissions from the use of fossil fuels growing at a rate of 1.9% per year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rogner_summary_of_emission_trends_99-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-rogner_summary_of_emission_trends-99">
        [99]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
       <a href="#cite_note-100">
        [100]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Total anthropogenic emissions at the end of 2009 were estimated at 49.5
      <a href="/wiki/Giga-" title="Giga-">
       gigatonnes
      </a>
      CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      -equivalent.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bridging_the_Emissions_Gap_101-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Bridging_the_Emissions_Gap-101">
        [101]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :15
      </sup>
      These emissions include CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      from fossil fuel use and from land use, as well as emissions of methane, nitrous oxide and other GHGs covered by the
      <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol" title="Kyoto Protocol">
       Kyoto Protocol
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      At present, the primary source of CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      emissions is the burning of
      <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">
       coal
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">
       natural gas
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">
       petroleum
      </a>
      for electricity and heat.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
       <a href="#cite_note-102">
        [102]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_and_national_attribution_of_emissions">
       Regional and national attribution of emissions
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Regional and national attribution of emissions">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol_and_government_action" title="Kyoto Protocol and government action">
       Kyoto Protocol and government action
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greenhouse_Gas_by_Sector.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="603" data-file-width="650" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Greenhouse_Gas_by_Sector.png/220px-Greenhouse_Gas_by_Sector.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Greenhouse_Gas_by_Sector.png/330px-Greenhouse_Gas_by_Sector.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Greenhouse_Gas_by_Sector.png/440px-Greenhouse_Gas_by_Sector.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greenhouse_Gas_by_Sector.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        This figure shows the relative fraction of anthropogenic greenhouse gases coming from each of eight categories of sources, as estimated by the Emission Database for Global Atmospheric Research version 3.2, fast track 2000 project [1]. These values are intended to provide a snapshot of global annual greenhouse gas emissions in the year 2000. The top panel shows the sum over all anthropogenic greenhouse gases, weighted by their global warming potential over the next 100 years. This consists of 72% carbon dioxide, 18% methane, 8% nitrous oxide and 1% other gases. Lower panels show the comparable information for each of these three primary greenhouse gases, with the same coloring of sectors as used in the top chart. Segments with less than 1% fraction are not labeled.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      There are several different ways of measuring GHG emissions, for example, see World Bank (2010)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-world_bank_emissions_data_103-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-world_bank_emissions_data-103">
        [103]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :362
      </sup>
      for tables of national emissions data. Some variables that have been reported
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sapiens.revues.org_104-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-sapiens.revues.org-104">
        [104]
       </a>
      </sup>
      include:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Definition of measurement boundaries: Emissions can be attributed geographically, to the area where they were emitted (the territory principle) or by the activity principle to the territory produced the emissions. These two principles result in different totals when measuring, for example, electricity importation from one country to another, or emissions at an international airport.
      </li>
      <li>
       Time horizon of different GHGs: Contribution of a given GHG is reported as a CO
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       equivalent. The calculation to determine this takes into account how long that gas remains in the atmosphere. This is not always known accurately and calculations must be regularly updated to reflect new information.
      </li>
      <li>
       What sectors are included in the calculation (e.g., energy industries, industrial processes, agriculture etc.): There is often a conflict between transparency and availability of data.
      </li>
      <li>
       The measurement protocol itself: This may be via direct measurement or estimation. The four main methods are the emission factor-based method, mass balance method, predictive emissions monitoring systems, and continuous emissions monitoring systems. These methods differ in accuracy, cost, and usability.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      These different measures are sometimes used by different countries to assert various policy/ethical positions on climate change (Banuri
      <i>
       et al.
      </i>
      , 1996, p. 94).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-banuri_105-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-banuri-105">
        [105]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This use of different measures leads to a lack of comparability, which is problematic when monitoring progress towards targets. There are arguments for the adoption of a common measurement tool, or at least the development of communication between different tools.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sapiens.revues.org_104-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-sapiens.revues.org-104">
        [104]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Emissions may be measured over long time periods. This measurement type is called historical or cumulative emissions. Cumulative emissions give some indication of who is responsible for the build-up in the atmospheric concentration of GHGs (IEA, 2007, p. 199).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iea_106-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-iea-106">
        [106]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The national accounts balance would be positively related to carbon emissions. The national accounts balance shows the difference between exports and imports. For many richer nations, such as the United States, the accounts balance is negative because more goods are imported than they are exported. This is mostly due to the fact that it is cheaper to produce goods outside of developed countries, leading the economies of developed countries to become increasingly dependent on services and not goods. We believed that a positive accounts balance would means that more production was occurring in a country, so more factories working would increase carbon emission levels.(Holtz-Eakin, 1995, pp.;85;101).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-holtz-eakin_107-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-holtz-eakin-107">
        [107]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Emissions may also be measured across shorter time periods. Emissions changes may, for example, be measured against a base year of 1990. 1990 was used in the
      <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change" title="United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change">
       United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change
      </a>
      (UNFCCC) as the base year for emissions, and is also used in the
      <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol" title="Kyoto Protocol">
       Kyoto Protocol
      </a>
      (some gases are also measured from the year 1995).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grubb_kyoto_protocol_85-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-grubb_kyoto_protocol-85">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :146,149
      </sup>
      A country's emissions may also be reported as a proportion of global emissions for a particular year.
     </p>
     <p>
      Another measurement is of per capita emissions. This divides a country's total annual emissions by its mid-year population.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-world_bank_emissions_data_103-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-world_bank_emissions_data-103">
        [103]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :370
      </sup>
      Per capita emissions may be based on historical or annual emissions (Banuri
      <i>
       et al.
      </i>
      , 1996, pp. 106–107).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-banuri_105-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-banuri-105">
        [105]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Land-use_change">
       Land-use change
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Land-use change">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture,_forestry_and_other_land_use,_1970-2010.png">
        <img alt="Refer to caption." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="671" data-file-width="1200" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture%2C_forestry_and_other_land_use%2C_1970-2010.png/220px-Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture%2C_forestry_and_other_land_use%2C_1970-2010.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture%2C_forestry_and_other_land_use%2C_1970-2010.png/330px-Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture%2C_forestry_and_other_land_use%2C_1970-2010.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture%2C_forestry_and_other_land_use%2C_1970-2010.png/440px-Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture%2C_forestry_and_other_land_use%2C_1970-2010.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture,_forestry_and_other_land_use,_1970-2010.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Greenhouse gas emissions from agriculture, forestry and other land use, 1970–2010.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Land-use change, e.g., the clearing of forests for agricultural use, can affect the concentration of GHGs in the atmosphere by altering how much carbon flows out of the atmosphere into
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sink" title="Carbon sink">
       carbon sinks
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
       <a href="#cite_note-108">
        [108]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Accounting for land-use change can be understood as an attempt to measure "net" emissions, i.e., gross emissions from all GHG sources minus the removal of emissions from the atmosphere by carbon sinks (Banuri
      <i>
       et al.
      </i>
      , 1996, pp. 92–93).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-banuri_105-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-banuri-105">
        [105]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      There are substantial uncertainties in the measurement of net carbon emissions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
       <a href="#cite_note-109">
        [109]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Additionally, there is controversy over how carbon sinks should be allocated between different regions and over time (Banuri
      <i>
       et al.
      </i>
      , 1996, p. 93).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-banuri_105-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-banuri-105">
        [105]
       </a>
      </sup>
      For instance, concentrating on more recent changes in carbon sinks is likely to favour those regions that have deforested earlier, e.g., Europe.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Greenhouse_gas_intensity">
       Greenhouse gas intensity
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Greenhouse gas intensity">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_carbon_intensity" title="List of countries by carbon intensity">
       List of countries by carbon intensity
      </a>
     </div>

         <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_intensity_of_GDP_(using_MER)_for_different_regions,_1982-2011_(corrected).png">
           <img alt="Refer to caption." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="671" data-file-width="1200" height="95" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Carbon_intensity_of_GDP_%28using_MER%29_for_different_regions%2C_1982-2011_%28corrected%29.png/170px-Carbon_intensity_of_GDP_%28using_MER%29_for_different_regions%2C_1982-2011_%28corrected%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Carbon_intensity_of_GDP_%28using_MER%29_for_different_regions%2C_1982-2011_%28corrected%29.png/255px-Carbon_intensity_of_GDP_%28using_MER%29_for_different_regions%2C_1982-2011_%28corrected%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Carbon_intensity_of_GDP_%28using_MER%29_for_different_regions%2C_1982-2011_%28corrected%29.png/340px-Carbon_intensity_of_GDP_%28using_MER%29_for_different_regions%2C_1982-2011_%28corrected%29.png 2x" width="170"/>
          </a>
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           <div class="magnify">
            <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_intensity_of_GDP_(using_MER)_for_different_regions,_1982-2011_(corrected).png" title="Enlarge">
            </a>
           </div>
           Carbon intensity of GDP (using MER) for different regions, 1982–2011.
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      Greenhouse gas intensity is a ratio between greenhouse gas emissions and another metric, e.g., gross domestic product (GDP) or energy use. The terms "carbon intensity" and "
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emissions_intensity" title="Emissions intensity">
       emissions intensity
      </a>
      " are also sometimes used.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
       <a href="#cite_note-110">
        [110]
       </a>
      </sup>
      GHG intensities may be calculated using
      <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product#Cross-border_comparison" title="Gross domestic product">
       market exchange rates
      </a>
      (MER) or
      <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">
       purchasing power parity
      </a>
      (PPP) (Banuri
      <i>
       et al.
      </i>
      , 1996, p. 96).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-banuri_105-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-banuri-105">
        [105]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Calculations based on MER show large differences in intensities between developed and developing countries, whereas calculations based on PPP show smaller differences.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Cumulative_and_historical_emissions">
       Cumulative and historical emissions
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Cumulative and historical emissions">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:184px;max-width:184px">
       <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px">

         </a>
        </div>

         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EU-15" title="EU-15">

       </div>
       <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px">
        <div class="thumbimage">

         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         Regional trends in annual CO
         <sub>

         </sub>
         emissions from fuel combustion between 1971 and 2009.
        </div>
       </div>
       <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px">
        <div class="thumbimage">

         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         Regional trends in annual per capita CO
         <sub>
          2
         </sub>
         emissions from fuel combustion between 1971 and 2009.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Cumulative anthropogenic (i.e., human-emitted) emissions of CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      from fossil fuel use are a major cause of
      <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">
       global warming
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
       <a href="#cite_note-111">
        [111]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and give some indication of which countries have contributed most to human-induced climate change.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hohne_2010_regional_contribution_to_global_warming_112-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-hohne_2010_regional_contribution_to_global_warming-112">
        [112]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :15
      </sup>
     </p>
     <table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center">
      <caption>
       Top-5 historic CO
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       contributors by region over the years 1800 to 1988 (in %)
      </caption>
      <tr>
       <th>
        Region
       </th>
       <th>
        Industrial
        <br/>
        CO
        <sub>
         2
        </sub>
       </th>
       <th>
        Total
        <br/>
        CO
        <sub>
         2
        </sub>
       </th>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td style="text-align:left;">
        OECD North America
       </td>
       <td>
        33.2
       </td>
       <td>
        29.7
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td style="text-align:left;">
        OECD Europe
       </td>
       <td>
        26.1
       </td>
       <td>
        16.6
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td style="text-align:left;">
        Former USSR
       </td>
       <td>
        14.1
       </td>
       <td>
        12.5
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td style="text-align:left;">
        China
       </td>
       <td>
        5.5
       </td>
       <td>
        6.0
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td style="text-align:left;">
        Eastern Europe
       </td>
       <td>
        5.5
       </td>
       <td>
        4.8
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      The table above to the left is based on Banuri
      <i>
       et al.
      </i>
      (1996, p. 94).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-banuri_105-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-banuri-105">
        [105]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Overall, developed countries accounted for 83.8% of industrial CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      emissions over this time period, and 67.8% of total CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      emissions. Developing countries accounted for industrial CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      emissions of 16.2% over this time period, and 32.2% of total CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      emissions. The estimate of total CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      emissions includes
      <a href="/wiki/Biota_(ecology)" title="Biota (ecology)">
       biotic
      </a>
      carbon emissions, mainly from deforestation. Banuri
      <i>
       et al.
      </i>
      (1996, p. 94)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-banuri_105-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-banuri-105">
        [105]
       </a>
      </sup>
      calculated per capita cumulative emissions based on then-current population. The ratio in per capita emissions between industrialized countries and developing countries was estimated at more than 10 to 1.
     </p>
     <p>
      Including biotic emissions brings about the same controversy mentioned earlier regarding carbon sinks and land-use change (Banuri
      <i>
       et al.
      </i>
      , 1996, pp. 93–94).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-banuri_105-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-banuri-105">
        [105]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The actual calculation of net emissions is very complex, and is affected by how carbon sinks are allocated between regions and the dynamics of the climate system.
     </p>
     <p>
      Non-
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">
       OECD
      </a>
      countries accounted for 42% of cumulative energy-related CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      emissions between 1890–2007.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-world_energy_outlook_2009_113-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-world_energy_outlook_2009-113">
        [113]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :179–180
      </sup>
      Over this time period, the US accounted for 28% of emissions; the EU, 23%; Russia, 11%; China, 9%; other OECD countries, 5%; Japan, 4%; India, 3%; and the rest of the world, 18%.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-world_energy_outlook_2009_113-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-world_energy_outlook_2009-113">
        [113]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :179–180
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Changes_since_a_particular_base_year">
       Changes since a particular base year
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Changes since a particular base year">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol#Government_action_and_emissions" title="Kyoto Protocol">
       Kyoto Protocol § Government action and emissions
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Between 1970–2004, global growth in annual CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      emissions was driven by North America, Asia, and the Middle East.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114">
       <a href="#cite_note-114">
        [114]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The sharp acceleration in CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      emissions since 2000 to more than a 3% increase per year (more than 2 ppm per year) from 1.1% per year during the 1990s is attributable to the lapse of formerly declining trends in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_intensity" title="Carbon intensity">
       carbon intensity
      </a>
      of both developing and developed nations. China was responsible for most of global growth in emissions during this period. Localised plummeting emissions associated with the collapse of the Soviet Union have been followed by slow emissions growth in this region due to more
      <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">
       efficient energy use
      </a>
      , made necessary by the increasing proportion of it that is exported.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raupach_83-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Raupach-83">
        [83]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In comparison, methane has not increased appreciably, and
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       N
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
       O
      </span>
      by 0.25% y
      <sup>
       −1
      </sup>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Using different base years for measuring emissions has an effect on estimates of national contributions to global warming.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hohne_2010_regional_contribution_to_global_warming_112-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-hohne_2010_regional_contribution_to_global_warming-112">
        [112]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :17–18
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
       <a href="#cite_note-115">
        [115]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This can be calculated by dividing a country's highest contribution to global warming starting from a particular base year, by that country's minimum contribution to global warming starting from a particular base year. Choosing between different base years of 1750, 1900, 1950, and 1990 has a significant effect for most countries.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hohne_2010_regional_contribution_to_global_warming_112-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-hohne_2010_regional_contribution_to_global_warming-112">
        [112]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :17–18
      </sup>
      Within the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G8" title="G8">
       G8
      </a>
      group of countries, it is most significant for the UK, France and Germany. These countries have a long history of CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      emissions (see the section on
      <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas#Cumulative_and_historical_emissions" title="Greenhouse gas">
       Cumulative and historical emissions
      </a>
      ).
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Annual_emissions">
       Annual emissions
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Annual emissions">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GHG_per_capita_2000.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="477" data-file-width="940" height="91" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/GHG_per_capita_2000.svg/180px-GHG_per_capita_2000.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/GHG_per_capita_2000.svg/270px-GHG_per_capita_2000.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/GHG_per_capita_2000.svg/360px-GHG_per_capita_2000.svg.png 2x" width="180"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GHG_per_capita_2000.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Per capita anthropogenic greenhouse gas emissions by country for the year 2000 including land-use change.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Annual per capita emissions in the industrialized countries are typically as much as ten times the average in developing countries.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grubb_kyoto_protocol_85-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-grubb_kyoto_protocol-85">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :144
      </sup>
      Due to China's fast economic development, its annual per capita emissions are quickly approaching the levels of those in the
      <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol#2012_emission_targets_and_.22flexible_mechanisms.22" title="Kyoto Protocol">
       Annex I group
      </a>
      of the Kyoto Protocol (i.e., the developed countries excluding the USA).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbl_annual_emissions_in_2008_116-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pbl_annual_emissions_in_2008-116">
        [116]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other countries with fast growing emissions are
      <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">
       South Korea
      </a>
      , Iran, and Australia (which apart from the oil rich Persian Gulf states, now has the highest percapita emission rate in the world). On the other hand, annual per capita emissions of the EU-15 and the USA are gradually decreasing over time.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbl_annual_emissions_in_2008_116-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-pbl_annual_emissions_in_2008-116">
        [116]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Emissions in Russia and
      <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">
       Ukraine
      </a>
      have decreased fastest since 1990 due to economic restructuring in these countries.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
       <a href="#cite_note-117">
        [117]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Energy statistics for fast growing economies are less accurate than those for the industrialized countries. For China's annual emissions in 2008, the
      <a href="/wiki/Netherlands_Environmental_Assessment_Agency" title="Netherlands Environmental Assessment Agency">
       Netherlands Environmental Assessment Agency
      </a>
      estimated an uncertainty range of about 10%.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbl_annual_emissions_in_2008_116-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-pbl_annual_emissions_in_2008-116">
        [116]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/GHG_footprint" title="GHG footprint">
       GHG footprint
      </a>
      , or greenhouse gas footprint, refers to the amount of GHG that are emitted during the creation of products or services. It is more comprehensive than the commonly used
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_footprint" title="Carbon footprint">
       carbon footprint
      </a>
      , which measures only carbon dioxide, one of many greenhouse gases.
     </p>
     <p>
      2015 was the first year to see both total global economic growth and a reduction of carbon emissions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118">
       <a href="#cite_note-118">
        [118]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Top_emitter_countries">
       Top emitter countries
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Top emitter countries">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ghg-co2-2012.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="680" height="463" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Ghg-co2-2012.svg/350px-Ghg-co2-2012.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Ghg-co2-2012.svg/525px-Ghg-co2-2012.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Ghg-co2-2012.svg/700px-Ghg-co2-2012.svg.png 2x" width="350"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ghg-co2-2012.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The top 40 countries emitting all greenhouse gases, showing both that derived from all sources including land clearance and forestry and also the CO2 component excluding those sources. Per capita figures are included. Data taken from
        <a class="external text" href="http://www.wri.org/resources/data-sets/cait-historical-emissions-data-countries-us-states-unfccc" rel="nofollow">
         World Resources Institute
        </a>
        , Washington. Note that Indonesia and Brazil show very much higher than on graphs simply showing fossil fuel use.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_carbon_dioxide_emissions" title="List of countries by carbon dioxide emissions">
       List of countries by carbon dioxide emissions
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_carbon_dioxide_emissions_per_capita" title="List of countries by carbon dioxide emissions per capita">
       List of countries by carbon dioxide emissions per capita
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_greenhouse_gas_emissions" title="List of countries by greenhouse gas emissions">
       List of countries by greenhouse gas emissions
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_greenhouse_gas_emissions_per_capita" title="List of countries by greenhouse gas emissions per capita">
       List of countries by greenhouse gas emissions per capita
      </a>
     </div>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Annual">

      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">

       </span>
