     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Embedded_emissions">
       Embedded emissions
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Embedded emissions">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      One way of attributing greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions is to measure the
      <a href="/wiki/Embedded_emissions" title="Embedded emissions">
       embedded emissions
      </a>
      (also referred to as "embodied emissions") of goods that are being consumed. Emissions are usually measured according to production, rather than consumption.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120">
       <a href="#cite_note-120">
        [120]
       </a>
      </sup>
      For example, in the main international
      <a href="/wiki/Treaty" title="Treaty">
       treaty
      </a>
      on climate change (the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNFCCC" title="UNFCCC">
       UNFCCC
      </a>
      ), countries report on emissions produced within their borders, e.g., the emissions produced from burning fossil fuels.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-world_energy_outlook_2009_113-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-world_energy_outlook_2009-113">
        [113]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :179
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis_consumption_emissions_121-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-davis_consumption_emissions-121">
        [121]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :1
      </sup>
      Under a production-based accounting of emissions, embedded emissions on imported goods are attributed to the exporting, rather than the importing, country. Under a consumption-based accounting of emissions, embedded emissions on imported goods are attributed to the importing country, rather than the exporting, country.
     </p>
     <p>
      Davis and Caldeira (2010)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis_consumption_emissions_121-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-davis_consumption_emissions-121">
        [121]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :4
      </sup>
      found that a substantial proportion of CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      emissions are traded internationally. The net effect of trade was to export emissions from China and other emerging markets to consumers in the US, Japan, and Western Europe. Based on annual emissions data from the year 2004, and on a per-capita consumption basis, the top-5 emitting countries were found to be (in tCO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      per person, per year): Luxembourg (34.7), the US (22.0), Singapore (20.2), Australia (16.7), and Canada (16.6).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis_consumption_emissions_121-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-davis_consumption_emissions-121">
        [121]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :5
      </sup>
      Carbon Trust research revealed that approximately 25% of all CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      emissions from human activities 'flow' (i.e. are imported or exported) from one country to another. Major developed economies were found to be typically net importers of embodied carbon emissions — with UK consumption emissions 34% higher than production emissions, and Germany (29%), Japan (19%) and the USA (13%) also significant net importers of embodied emissions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">
       <a href="#cite_note-122">
        [122]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_of_policy">
       Effect of policy
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Effect of policy">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol_and_government_action" title="Kyoto Protocol and government action">
       Kyoto Protocol and government action
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Governments have taken action to reduce GHG emissions (
      <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_mitigation" title="Climate change mitigation">
       climate change mitigation
      </a>
      ). Assessments of policy effectiveness have included work by the
      <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">
       Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123">
       <a href="#cite_note-123">
        [123]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">
       International Energy Agency
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124">
       <a href="#cite_note-124">
        [124]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125">
       <a href="#cite_note-125">
        [125]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Environment_Programme" title="United Nations Environment Programme">
       United Nations Environment Programme
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126">
       <a href="#cite_note-126">
        [126]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Policies implemented by governments have included
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127">
       <a href="#cite_note-127">
        [127]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128">
       <a href="#cite_note-128">
        [128]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications_129-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications-129">
        [129]
       </a>
      </sup>
      national and regional targets to reduce emissions, promoting
      <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">
       energy efficiency
      </a>
      , and support for
      <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">
       renewable energy
      </a>
      such as Solar energy as an effective use of renewable energy because solar uses energy from the sun and does not release pollutants into the air.
     </p>
     <p>
      Countries and regions listed in Annex I of the
      <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change" title="United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change">
       United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change
      </a>
      (UNFCCC) (i.e., the OECD and former planned economies of the Soviet Union) are required to submit periodic assessments to the UNFCCC of actions they are taking to address climate change.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications_129-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications-129">
        [129]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :3
      </sup>
      Analysis by the UNFCCC (2011)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications_129-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications-129">
        [129]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :8
      </sup>
      suggested that policies and measures undertaken by Annex I Parties may have produced emission savings of 1.5 thousand
      <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude_(mass)" title="Orders of magnitude (mass)">
       Tg
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">
       CO
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       -eq
      </a>
      in the year 2010, with most savings made in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_sector" title="Energy sector">
       energy sector
      </a>
      . The projected emissions saving of 1.5 thousand Tg CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      -eq is measured against a hypothetical "
      <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_climate_change_mitigation#Baselines" title="Economics of climate change mitigation">
       baseline
      </a>
      " of Annex I emissions, i.e., projected Annex I emissions in the absence of policies and measures. The total projected Annex I saving of 1.5 thousand CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      -eq does not include emissions savings in seven of the Annex I Parties.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications_129-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications-129">
        [129]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :8
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Projections">
       Projections
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Projections">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Further information:
      <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_scenario#Quantitative_emissions_projections" title="Climate change scenario">
       climate change scenario § Quantitative emissions projections
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_climate_model#Projections_of_future_climate_change" title="Global climate model">
       Global climate model § Projections of future climate change
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      A wide range of projections of future GHG emissions have been produced.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130">
       <a href="#cite_note-130">
        [130]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Rogner
      <i>
       et al.
      </i>
      (2007)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131">
       <a href="#cite_note-131">
        [131]
       </a>
      </sup>
      assessed the scientific literature on GHG projections. Rogner
      <i>
       et al.
      </i>
      (2007)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rogner_summary_of_emission_trends_99-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-rogner_summary_of_emission_trends-99">
        [99]
       </a>
      </sup>
      concluded that unless energy policies changed substantially, the world would continue to depend on fossil fuels until 2025–2030. Projections suggest that more than 80% of the world's energy will come from fossil fuels. This conclusion was based on "much evidence" and "high agreement" in the literature.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rogner_summary_of_emission_trends_99-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-rogner_summary_of_emission_trends-99">
        [99]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Projected annual energy-related CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      emissions in 2030 were 40–110% higher than in 2000, with two-thirds of the increase originating in developing countries.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rogner_summary_of_emission_trends_99-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-rogner_summary_of_emission_trends-99">
        [99]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Projected annual per capita emissions in developed country regions remained substantially lower (2.8–5.1
      <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">
       tonnes
      </a>
      CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      ) than those in developed country regions (9.6–15.1 tonnes CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132">
       <a href="#cite_note-132">
        [132]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Projections consistently showed increase in annual world GHG emissions (the
      <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol" title="Kyoto Protocol">
       "Kyoto"
      </a>
      gases,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133">
       <a href="#cite_note-133">
        [133]
       </a>
      </sup>
      measured in
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">
       CO
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       -equivalent
      </a>
      ) of 25–90% by 2030, compared to 2000.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rogner_summary_of_emission_trends_99-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-rogner_summary_of_emission_trends-99">
        [99]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Relative_CO2_emission_from_various_fuels">
       Relative CO
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       emission from various fuels
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Relative CO2 emission from various fuels">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      One liter of gasoline, when used as a fuel, produces
      <span class="nowrap">
       2.32 kg
      </span>
      (about 1300 liters or 1.3 cubic meters) of carbon dioxide, a greenhouse gas. One US gallon produces 19.4 lb (1,291.5 gallons or 172.65 cubic feet)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134">
       <a href="#cite_note-134">
        [134]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135">
       <a href="#cite_note-135">
        [135]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136">
       <a href="#cite_note-136">
        [136]
       </a>
      </sup>

       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">

       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Life-cycle_greenhouse-gas_emissions_of_energy_sources" title="Life-cycle greenhouse-gas emissions of energy sources">

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Removal_from_the_atmosphere_.28.22sinks.22.29">
       Removal from the atmosphere ("sinks")
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title='Edit section: Removal from the atmosphere ("sinks")'>
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_processes">
       Natural processes
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Natural processes">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Greenhouse gases can be removed from the atmosphere by various processes, as a consequence of:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       a physical change (condensation and precipitation remove water vapor from the atmosphere).
      </li>
      <li>
       a chemical reaction within the atmosphere. For example, methane is
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidized" title="Oxidized">
        oxidized
       </a>
       by reaction with naturally occurring
       <a href="/wiki/Hydroxyl_radical" title="Hydroxyl radical">
        hydroxyl radical
       </a>
       , OH
       <b>
        ·
       </b>
       and degraded to CO
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       and water vapor (CO
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       from the oxidation of methane is not included in the methane
       <a href="/wiki/Global_warming_potential" title="Global warming potential">
        Global warming potential
       </a>
       ). Other chemical reactions include solution and solid phase chemistry occurring in atmospheric aerosols.
      </li>
      <li>
       a physical exchange between the atmosphere and the other compartments of the planet. An example is the mixing of atmospheric gases into the oceans.
      </li>
      <li>
       a chemical change at the interface between the atmosphere and the other compartments of the planet. This is the case for CO
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       , which is reduced by
       <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">
        photosynthesis
       </a>
       of plants, and which, after dissolving in the oceans, reacts to form
       <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_acid" title="Carbonic acid">
        carbonic acid
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Bicarbonate" title="Bicarbonate">
        bicarbonate
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">
        carbonate
       </a>
       ions (see
       <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">
        ocean acidification
       </a>
       ).
      </li>
      <li>
       a
       <a href="/wiki/Photochemistry" title="Photochemistry">
        photochemical change
       </a>
       .
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halocarbons" title="Halocarbons">
        Halocarbons
       </a>
       are dissociated by
       <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">
        UV
       </a>
       light releasing Cl
       <b>
        ·
       </b>
       and F
       <b>
        ·
       </b>
       as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_radical" title="Free radical">
        free radicals
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">
        stratosphere
       </a>
       with harmful effects on
       <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">
        ozone
       </a>
       (halocarbons are generally too stable to disappear by chemical reaction in the atmosphere).
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Negative_emissions">
       Negative emissions
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Negative emissions">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Negative_carbon_dioxide_emission" title="Negative carbon dioxide emission">
       Negative carbon dioxide emission
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      A number of technologies remove greenhouse gases emissions from the atmosphere. Most widely analysed are those that remove carbon dioxide from the atmosphere, either to geologic formations such as
      <a href="/wiki/Bio-energy_with_carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Bio-energy with carbon capture and storage">
       bio-energy with carbon capture and storage
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139">
       <a href="#cite_note-139">
        [139]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140">
       <a href="#cite_note-140">
        [140]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoyalSociety_141-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-RoyalSociety-141">
        [141]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_air_capture" title="Carbon dioxide air capture">
       carbon dioxide air capture
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoyalSociety_141-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-RoyalSociety-141">
        [141]
       </a>
      </sup>
      or to the soil as in the case with
      <a href="/wiki/Biochar" title="Biochar">
       biochar
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoyalSociety_141-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-RoyalSociety-141">
        [141]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The IPCC has pointed out that many long-term climate scenario models require large scale manmade negative emissions to avoid serious climate change.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC2007_142-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-IPCC2007-142">
        [142]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_scientific_research">
       History of scientific research
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: History of scientific research">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      In the late 19th century scientists experimentally discovered that
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       N
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      and
      <span class="chemf nowrap">
       O
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      do not absorb infrared radiation (called, at that time, "dark radiation"), while water (both as true vapor and condensed in the form of microscopic droplets suspended in clouds) and CO
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      and other poly-atomic gaseous molecules do absorb infrared radiation. In the early 20th century researchers realized that greenhouse gases in the atmosphere made Earth's overall temperature higher than it would be without them. During the late 20th century, a
      <a href="/wiki/Scientific_opinion_on_climate_change" title="Scientific opinion on climate change">
       scientific consensus
      </a>
      evolved that increasing concentrations of greenhouse gases in the atmosphere cause a substantial rise in global temperatures and changes to other parts of the climate system,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143">
       <a href="#cite_note-143">
        [143]
       </a>
      </sup>
      with
      <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming" title="Effects of global warming">
       consequences
      </a>
      for the
      <a href="/wiki/Physical_impacts_of_climate_change" title="Physical impacts of climate change">
       environment
      </a>
      and for
      <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming_on_human_health" title="Effects of global warming on human health">
       human health
      </a>
      .
     </p>

       <li id="cite_note-blasing_pre_1750_ghg_conc-49">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         ^
         <a href="#cite_ref-blasing_pre_1750_ghg_conc_49-0">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             a
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
         <a href="#cite_ref-blasing_pre_1750_ghg_conc_49-1">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             b
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
         <a href="#cite_ref-blasing_pre_1750_ghg_conc_49-2">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             c
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <cite class="citation" id="CITEREFEhhalt.2C_D.">
          Ehhalt, D.; et al., "Ch 4. Atmospheric Chemistry and Greenhouse Gases",
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.grida.no/climate/ipcc_tar/wg1/130.htm" rel="nofollow">
           <i>
            Table 4.1
           </i>
          </a>
         </cite>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGreenhouse+gas&amp;rft.atitle=Ch+4.+Atmospheric+Chemistry+and+Greenhouse+Gases&amp;rft.au=Ehhalt%2C+D.&amp;rft.btitle=Table+4.1&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.grida.no%2Fclimate%2Fipcc_tar%2Fwg1%2F130.htm&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
         , in
         <a href="#CITEREFIPCC_TAR_WG12001">
          IPCC TAR WG1 2001
         </a>
         , pp. 244–245. Referred to by:
         <cite class="citation" id="CITEREFBlasing.2C_T._J.2013">
          Blasing, T. J. (February 2013),
          <a class="external text" href="http://cdiac.ornl.gov/pns/current_ghg.html" rel="nofollow">
           <i>
            Current Greenhouse Gas Concentrations
           </i>
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">
           doi
          </a>
          :
          <a class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.3334%2FCDIAC%2Fatg.032" rel="nofollow">
           10.3334/CDIAC/atg.032
          </a>
         </cite>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGreenhouse+gas&amp;rft.au=Blasing%2C+T.+J.&amp;rft.btitle=Current+Greenhouse+Gas+Concentrations&amp;rft.date=2013-02&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fcdiac.ornl.gov%2Fpns%2Fcurrent_ghg.html&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.3334%2FCDIAC%2Fatg.032&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
         , on
         <a href="#CITEREFCDIAC2013">
          CDIAC 2013
         </a>
         . Based on Blasing
         <i>
          et al.
         </i>
         (2013): Pre-1750 concentrations of CH4,N2O and current concentrations of O3, are taken from Table 4.1 (a) of the IPCC Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change), 2001. Following the convention of IPCC (2001), inferred global-scale trace-gas concentrations from prior to 1750 are assumed to be practically uninfluenced by human activities such as increasingly specialized
         <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">
          agriculture
         </a>
         ,
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Land_clearing" title="Land clearing">
          land clearing
         </a>
         , and combustion of fossil fuels. Preindustrial concentrations of industrially manufactured compounds are given as zero. The short atmospheric lifetime of ozone (hours-days) together with the spatial variability of its sources precludes a globally or vertically homogeneous distribution, so that a fractional unit such as parts per billion would not apply over a range of altitudes or geographical locations. Therefore a different unit is used to integrate the varying concentrations of ozone in the vertical dimension over a unit area, and the results can then be averaged globally. This unit is called a
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dobson_Unit" title="Dobson Unit">
          Dobson Unit
         </a>
         (D.U.), after G. M. B. Dobson, one of the first investigators of atmospheric ozone. A Dobson unit is the amount of ozone in a column that, unmixed with the rest of the atmosphere, would be 10 micrometers thick at standard temperature and pressure.
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-50">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-50">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Because atmospheric concentrations of most gases tend to vary systematically over the course of a year, figures given represent averages over a 12-month period for all gases except ozone (O3), for which a current global value has been estimated (IPCC, 2001, Table 4.1a). CO
         <sub>
          2
         </sub>
         averages for year 2012 are taken from the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration, Earth System Research Laboratory, web site: www.esrl.noaa.gov/gmd/ccgg/trends maintained by Dr. Pieter Tans. For other chemical species, the values given are averages for 2011. These data are found on the CDIAC AGAGE web site:
         <a class="external free" href="http://cdiac.ornl.gov/ndps/alegage.html" rel="nofollow">
          http://cdiac.ornl.gov/ndps/alegage.html
         </a>
         or the AGAGE home page:
         <a class="external free" href="http://agage.eas.gatech.edu" rel="nofollow">
          http://agage.eas.gatech.edu
         </a>
         .
        </span>

         Changes in
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stratospheric" title="Stratospheric">

         </a>
